Austin</a>.</div>\n<p class="mw-empty-elt">\n\n\n\n</p>\n<table class="infobox vcard" style="width:22em"><tbody><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;font-size:125%;font-weight:bold"><span /></a></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Jane Austen</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/\xcb\x88/: primary stress follows">\xcb\x88</span><span title="/\xc9\x92/: &#39;o&#39; in &#39;body&#39;">\xc9\x92</span><span title="&#39;s&#39; in &#39;sigh&#39;">s</span><span title="&#39;t&#39; in &#39;tie&#39;">t</span><span title="/\xc9\xaa/: &#39;i&#39; in &#39;kit&#39;">\xc9\xaa</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span></span>, <wbr /><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/\xcb\x88/: primary stress follows">\xcb\x88</span><span title="/\xc9\x94\xcb\x90/: &#39;au&#39; in &#39;fraud&#39;">\xc9\x94\xcb\x90</span><span title="&#39;s&#39; in &#39;sigh&#39;">s</span></span>-/</a></span></span>; 16 December 1775&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 18 July 1817) was an English novelist known primarily for her six major novels, which interpret, critique and comment upon the British <a href="/wiki/Landed_gentry" title="Landed gentry">landed gentry</a> at the end of the 18th century. Austen\'s plots often explore the dependence of women on marriage in the pursuit of favourable social standing and economic security. Her works critique the <a href="/wiki/Sentimental_novel" title="Sentimental novel">novels of sensibility</a> of the second half of the 18th century and are part of the transition to 19th-century literary realism.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;b&#93;</a></sup> Her use of biting irony, along with her realism and social commentary, have earned her acclaim among critics and scholars.\n</p><p>With the publications of <i><a href="/wiki/Sense_and_Sensibility" title="Sense and Sensibility">Sense and Sensibility</a></i> (1811),  <i><a href="/wiki/Pride_and_Prejudice" title="Pride and Prejudice">Pride and Prejudice</a></i> (1813), <i><a href="/wiki/Mansfield_Park" title="Mansfield Park">Mansfield Park</a></i> (1814) and <i><a href="/wiki/Emma_(novel)" title="Emma (novel)">Emma</a></i> (1816), she achieved success as a published writer. She wrote two additional novels, <i><a href="/wiki/Northanger_Abbey" title="Northanger Abbey">Northanger Abbey</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Persuasion_(novel)" title="Persuasion (novel)">Persuasion</a></i>, both published posthumously in 1818, and began another, eventually titled <i><a href="/wiki/Sanditon" title="Sanditon">Sanditon</a></i>, but died before its completion. She also left behind three volumes of juvenile writings in manuscript, a short <a href="/wiki/Epistolary_novel" title="Epistolary novel">epistolary novel</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Lady_Susan" title="Lady Susan">Lady Susan</a></i>, and another unfinished novel, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Watsons" title="The Watsons">The Watsons</a></i>. Her six full-length novels have rarely been out of print, although they were published anonymously and brought her moderate success and little fame during her lifetime.\n</p><p>A significant transition in her posthumous reputation occurred in 1833, when her novels were republished in Richard Bentley\'s Standard Novels series, illustrated by Ferdinand Pickering, and sold as a set.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> They gradually gained wider acclaim and popular readership. In 1869, fifty-two years after her death, her nephew\'s publication of <i><a href="/wiki/A_Memoir_of_Jane_Austen" title="A Memoir of Jane Austen">A Memoir of Jane Austen</a></i> introduced a compelling version of her writing career and supposedly uneventful life to an eager audience.\n</p><p>Austen has inspired a large number of critical essays and literary anthologies. Her novels have inspired many films, from 1940\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Pride_and_Prejudice_(1940_film)" title="Pride and Prejudice (1940 film)">Pride and Prejudice</a></i> to more recent productions like <i><a href="/wiki/Sense_and_Sensibility_(film)" title="Sense and Sensibility (film)">Sense and Sensibility</a></i> (1995), <i><a href="/wiki/Emma_(1996_theatrical_film)" title="Emma (1996 theatrical film)">Emma</a></i> (1996), <i><a href="/wiki/Mansfield_Park_(film)" title="Mansfield Park (film)">Mansfield Park</a></i> (1999), <i><a href="/wiki/Pride_%26_Prejudice_(2005_film)" title="Pride &amp; Prejudice (2005 film)">Pride &amp; Prejudice</a></i> (2005), and  <i><a href="/wiki/Love_%26_Friendship" title="Love &amp; Friendship">Love &amp; Friendship</a></i> (2016).\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-24"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">10.1</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-25"><a href="#Citations"><span class="tocnumber">10.2</span> <span class="toctext">Citations</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-26"><a href="#Sources"><span class="tocnumber">10.3</span> <span class="toctext">Sources</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-27"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-28"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-29"><a href="#Museums"><span class="tocnumber">12.1</span> <span class="toctext">Museums</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-30"><a href="#Fan_sites_and_societies"><span class="tocnumber">12.2</span> <span class="toctext">Fan sites and societies</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biographical_sources">Biographical sources</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Letter_from_Jane_Austen_to_her_sister_Cassandra,_1799_June_11._Page_4_(NLA).tiff" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Letter_from_Jane_Austen_to_her_sister_Cassandra%2C_1799_June_11._Page_4_%28NLA%29.tiff/lossy-page1-200px-Letter_from_Jane_Austen_to_her_sister_Cassandra%2C_1799_June_11._Page_4_%28NLA%29.tiff.jpg" width="200" height="246" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Letter_from_Jane_Austen_to_her_sister_Cassandra%2C_1799_June_11._Page_4_%28NLA%29.tiff/lossy-page1-300px-Letter_from_Jane_Austen_to_her_sister_Cassandra%2C_1799_June_11._Page_4_%28NLA%29.tiff.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Letter_from_Jane_Austen_to_her_sister_Cassandra%2C_1799_June_11._Page_4_%28NLA%29.tiff/lossy-page1-400px-Letter_from_Jane_Austen_to_her_sister_Cassandra%2C_1799_June_11._Page_4_%28NLA%29.tiff.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5472" data-file-height="6728" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Letter_from_Jane_Austen_to_her_sister_Cassandra,_1799_June_11._Page_4_(NLA).tiff" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Last page of letter from Austen to her sister, Cassandra, 11 June 1799</div></div></div>\n<p>There is little biographical information about Jane Austen\'s life except the few letters that survive and the biographical notes her family members wrote.<sup id="cite_ref-Fergus(2005)3ff_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fergus(2005)3ff-7">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> During her lifetime, Austen wrote approximately 3,000 letters but only about 160 survived.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Many of the letters were written to Austen\'s older sister <a href="/wiki/Cassandra_Austen" title="Cassandra Austen">Cassandra</a>, who in 1843 burned the greater part of them and cut pieces out of those she kept. Ostensibly, Cassandra destroyed or censored her sister\'s letters to prevent their falling into the hands of relatives and ensuring that "younger nieces did not read any of Jane Austen\'s sometimes acid or forthright comments on neighbours or family members".<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;c&#93;</a></sup> Cassandra believed that in the interest of tact and Jane\'s penchant for forthrightness, these details should be destroyed. The paucity of record of Austen\'s life leaves modern biographers little to work with.<sup id="cite_ref-Nokes_1998_1_2_Fergus_2005_3_4_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nokes_1998_1_2_Fergus_2005_3_4-10">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The situation was compounded as successive generations of the family expunged and sanitized the already opaque details of Austen\'s biography. The heirs of Jane\'s brother, Admiral <a href="/wiki/Francis_Austen" title="Francis Austen">Francis Austen</a>, destroyed more letters; details were excised from the "Biographical Notice" her brother wrote in 1818; and family details continued to be <span class="clarify-content" style="padding-left:0.1em; padding-right:0.1em; color:#595959; border:1px solid #DDD;">elided</span><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Clarify" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="what? (February 2018)">clarify</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> or embellished in her nephew\'s <i><a href="/wiki/A_Memoir_of_Jane_Austen" title="A Memoir of Jane Austen">A Memoir of Jane Austen</a></i>, published in 1869, and in William and Richard Arthur Austen-Leigh\'s biography <i>Jane Austen: Her Life and Letters</i>, published in 1913.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> The legend the family and relatives created reflects their biases in favour of "good quiet Aunt Jane", portraying a woman whose domestic situation was happy and whose family was the mainstay of her life.<sup id="cite_ref-Fergus(2005)3ff_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fergus(2005)3ff-7">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Austen scholar Jan Fergus explains that modern biographies tend to include details excised from the letters and family biographical materials, but that the challenge is to avoid the polarising view that Austen experienced periods of deep unhappiness and was "an embittered, disappointed woman trapped in a thoroughly unpleasant family".<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life">Life</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Jane_Austen" title="Timeline of Jane Austen">Timeline of Jane Austen</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Family">Family</span></h3>\n<p>Jane Austen was born in <a href="/wiki/Steventon,_Hampshire" title="Steventon, Hampshire">Steventon, Hampshire</a>, on 16 December 1775. She was born a month later than her parents expected; her father wrote of her arrival in a letter that her mother "certainly expected to have been brought to bed a month ago". He added that her arrival was particularly welcome as "a future companion to her sister".<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>  The winter of 1776 was particularly harsh and it was not until 5 April that she was baptised at the local church with the single name Jane.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>For much of Jane\'s life, her father, George Austen (1731\xe2\x80\x931805) served as the <a href="/wiki/Rector_(ecclesiastical)" title="Rector (ecclesiastical)">rector</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Anglican</a> <a href="/wiki/Parish" title="Parish">parishes</a> at Steventon and at nearby <a href="/wiki/Deane,_Hampshire" title="Deane, Hampshire">Deane</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;d&#93;</a></sup> He came from an old, respected, and wealthy family of wool merchants. Over the centuries as each generation of eldest sons received inheritances their wealth was consolidated, and George\'s branch of the family fell into poverty. He and his two sisters were  orphaned as children and had to be taken in by relatives. His sister Philadelphia went to India to find a husband and George entered <a href="/wiki/St_John%27s_College,_Oxford" title="St John&#39;s College, Oxford">St John\'s College, Oxford</a> on a fellowship, where he most likely met Cassandra Leigh (1739\xe2\x80\x931827).<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> She came from the prominent <a href="/wiki/Baron_Leigh" title="Baron Leigh">Leigh</a> family; her father was rector at <a href="/wiki/All_Souls_College,_Oxford" title="All Souls College, Oxford">All Souls College, Oxford</a>, where she grew up among the gentry. Her eldest brother James inherited a fortune and large estate from his great-aunt Perrot, with the only condition that he <a href="/wiki/Name_change#Historical_usage" title="Name change">change his name</a> to Leigh-Perrot.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> \n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Chawton_Church,_Steventon,_Hampshire.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Chawton_Church%2C_Steventon%2C_Hampshire.jpg/260px-Chawton_Church%2C_Steventon%2C_Hampshire.jpg" width="260" height="162" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6e/Chawton_Church%2C_Steventon%2C_Hampshire.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="386" data-file-height="240" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Chawton_Church,_Steventon,_Hampshire.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Steventon,_Hampshire" title="Steventon, Hampshire">Steventon</a> Church, as depicted in <i><a href="/wiki/A_Memoir_of_Jane_Austen" title="A Memoir of Jane Austen">A Memoir of Jane Austen</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-Le_Faye_2004_20_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Le_Faye_2004_20-21">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>George and Cassandra exchanged miniatures in 1763 and probably were engaged around that time.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> George received the <a href="/wiki/Benefice" title="Benefice">living</a> for the Steventon parish from the wealthy husband of his second cousin, Thomas Knight, who owned Steventon and its associated farms, one of which the Austen family rented to live in.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>  Two months after Cassandra\'s father died they married on 26 April 1764 at <a href="/wiki/Church_of_St_Swithin,_Bath" title="Church of St Swithin, Bath">St Swithin\'s Church</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bath,_Somerset" title="Bath, Somerset">Bath</a>, by licence, in a simple ceremony. They left for Hampshire the same day.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> \n</p><p>Their income was modest, with George\'s small <i>per annum</i> living; Cassandra brought to the marriage the expectation of a small inheritance at the time of her mother\'s death.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> The Austens took up temporary residence at the nearby Deane rectory until Steventon, a 16th century house in disrepair, underwent necessary renovations. Cassandra gave birth to three children while living at Deane: James in 1765, George in 1766, and <a href="/wiki/Edward_Austen_Knight" title="Edward Austen Knight">Edward</a> in 1767.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Her custom was to keep an infant at home for several months and then place it with Elizabeth Littlewood, a woman living nearby to <a href="/wiki/Wet_nurse" title="Wet nurse">nurse</a> and raise for twelve to eighteen months.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Steventon">Steventon</span></h3>\n<p>In 1768 the family finally took up residence in Steventon. <a href="/wiki/Henry_Thomas_Austen" title="Henry Thomas Austen">Henry</a> was the first child to be born there, in 1771.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> At about this time Cassandra could no longer ignore the signs that little George was developmentally disabled. He was subject to seizures, may have been deaf and dumb, and she chose to send him out to be fostered.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> In 1773, Cassandra was born, followed by <a href="/wiki/Francis_Austen" title="Francis Austen">Francis</a> in 1774, and Jane in 1775.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:SteventonRectory.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/SteventonRectory.jpg/220px-SteventonRectory.jpg" width="220" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/SteventonRectory.jpg/330px-SteventonRectory.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0a/SteventonRectory.jpg 2x" data-file-width="432" data-file-height="282" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:SteventonRectory.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Steventon,_Hampshire" title="Steventon, Hampshire">Steventon</a> rectory, as depicted in <i><a href="/wiki/A_Memoir_of_Jane_Austen" title="A Memoir of Jane Austen">A Memoir of Jane Austen</a></i>, was in a valley and surrounded by meadows.<sup id="cite_ref-Le_Faye_2004_20_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Le_Faye_2004_20-21">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>According to Honan, the atmosphere of the Austen home was an "open, amused, easy intellectual" one, where the ideas of those with whom the Austens might disagree politically or socially were considered and discussed.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> The family relied on the patronage of their kin and hosted visits from numerous family members.<sup id="cite_ref-Todd4_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Todd4-32">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Cassandra Austen spent the summer of 1770 in London with George\'s sister, Philadelphia, and her daughter <a href="/wiki/Eliza_de_Feuillide" title="Eliza de Feuillide">Eliza</a>, accompanied by his other sister, Mrs Walter and her daughter Philly.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;e&#93;</a></sup> Philadelphia and Eliza Hancock were, according to Le Faye, "the bright comets flashing into an otherwise placid solar system of clerical life in rural Hampshire, and the news of their foreign travels and fashionable London life, together with their sudden descents upon the Steventon household in between times, all helped to widen Jane\'s youthful horizon and influence her later life and works."<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Cassandra Austen\'s cousin Thomas Leigh visited a number of times in the 1770s and \'80s, inviting young Cassie to visit them in Bath in 1781. The first mention of Jane occurs in family documents on her return,  "\xe2\x80\xa6&#160;and almost home they were when they met Jane &amp; Charles, the two little ones of the family, who had to go as far as New Down to meet the chaise, &amp; have the pleasure of riding home in it."<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Le Faye writes that, "Mr Austen\'s predictions for his younger daughter were fully justified. Never were sisters more to each other than Cassandra and Jane; while in a particularly affectionate family, there seems to have been a special link between Cassandra and Edward on the one hand, and between Henry and Jane on the other."<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>From 1773 until 1796, George Austen supplemented his income by farming and by teaching three or four boys at a time, who boarded at his home.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> The Reverend Austen had an annual income of \xc2\xa3200 from his two livings.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> This was a very modest income at the time; by comparison, a skilled worker like a blacksmith or a carpenter could make about \xc2\xa3100 annually while the typical and annual income of a gentry family was between \xc2\xa31,000 and \xc2\xa35,000.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During this period of her life, Austen attended church regularly, socialized with friends and neighbours,<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;f&#93;</a></sup> and read novels \xe2\x80\x93 often of her own composition \xe2\x80\x93 aloud with her family in the evenings. Socialising with the neighbours often meant dancing, either impromptu in someone\'s home after supper or at the balls held regularly at the <a href="/wiki/Assembly_rooms" title="Assembly rooms">assembly rooms</a> in the town hall.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Her brother Henry later said that "Jane was fond of dancing, and excelled in it".<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:CassandraAustenSilhouette.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/CassandraAustenSilhouette.png/220px-CassandraAustenSilhouette.png" width="220" height="297" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/94/CassandraAustenSilhouette.png 1.5x" data-file-width="221" data-file-height="298" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:CassandraAustenSilhouette.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Silhouette of <a href="/wiki/Cassandra_Austen" title="Cassandra Austen">Cassandra Austen</a>, Jane\'s sister and closest friend</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1783, Austen and her sister Cassandra were sent to <a href="/wiki/Oxford" title="Oxford">Oxford</a> to be educated by Mrs Ann Cawley who took them with her to <a href="/wiki/Southampton" title="Southampton">Southampton</a> when she moved there later in the year. In the autumn both girls were sent home when they caught <a href="/wiki/Typhus" title="Typhus">typhus</a> and Austen nearly died.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> Austen was from then home educated, until she attended boarding school in <a href="/wiki/Reading,_Berkshire" title="Reading, Berkshire">Reading</a> with her sister from early in 1785 at the <a href="/wiki/Reading_Abbey_Girls%27_School" title="Reading Abbey Girls&#39; School">Reading Abbey Girls\' School</a>, ruled by Mrs La Tournelle, who possessed a cork leg and a passion for theatre.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> The school curriculum probably included some French, spelling, needlework, dancing and music and, perhaps, drama. The sisters returned home before December 1786 because the school fees for the two girls were too high for the Austen family.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>  After 1786, Austen "never again lived anywhere beyond the bounds of her immediate family environment".<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The remainder of her education came from reading, guided by her father and brothers James and Henry.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Irene Collins believes that Austen "used some of the same school books as the boys" her father tutored.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Austen apparently had unfettered access both to her father\'s library and that of a family friend, <a href="/wiki/Warren_Hastings" title="Warren Hastings">Warren Hastings</a>. Together these collections amounted to a large and varied library.  Her father was also tolerant of Austen\'s sometimes risqu\xc3\xa9 experiments in writing, and provided both sisters with expensive paper and other materials for their writing and drawing.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Private theatricals were an essential part of Austen\'s education. From her early childhood, the family and friends staged a series of plays in the rectory barn, including <a href="/wiki/Richard_Brinsley_Sheridan" title="Richard Brinsley Sheridan">Richard Sheridan</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Rivals" title="The Rivals">The Rivals</a></i> (1775) and <a href="/wiki/David_Garrick" title="David Garrick">David Garrick</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Bon_Ton_(play)" title="Bon Ton (play)">Bon Ton</a></i>. Austen\'s eldest brother James wrote the prologues and epilogues and she probably joined in these activities, first as a spectator and later as a participant.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Most of the plays were comedies, which suggests how Austen\'s satirical gifts were cultivated.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> At the age of 12, she tried her own hand at dramatic writing; she wrote three short plays during her teenage years.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Juvenilia_.281787.E2.80.931793.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Juvenilia_(1787\xe2\x80\x931793)">Juvenilia (1787\xe2\x80\x931793)</span></h3>\n<p>From the age of eleven, and perhaps earlier, Austen wrote poems and stories for her own and her family\'s amusement.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> In these works the details of daily life are exaggerated, common plot devices are parodied, and the "stories are full of anarchic fantasies of female power, licence, illicit behaviour, and general high spirits", according to <a href="/wiki/Janet_Todd" title="Janet Todd">Janet Todd</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Austen later<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (April 2018)">when?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> compiled <a href="/wiki/Foul_papers" title="Foul papers">fair copies</a> of twenty-nine early works into three bound notebooks, now referred to as the <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Juvenilia_(Austen)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Juvenilia (Austen) (page does not exist)">Juvenilia</a></i>, containing work written between 1787 and 1793. She titled the three notebooks \xe2\x80\x93 <i>Volume the First</i>, <i>Volume the Second</i> and <i>Volume the Third</i> \xe2\x80\x93 which preserve 90,000 words she wrote during those years.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> The <i>Juvenilia</i> are often, according to scholar Richard Jenkyns, "boisterous" and "anarchic"; he compares them to the work of 18th-century novelist <a href="/wiki/Laurence_Sterne" title="Laurence Sterne">Laurence Sterne</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:CassandraAusten-HenryIV.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/CassandraAusten-HenryIV.jpg/220px-CassandraAusten-HenryIV.jpg" width="220" height="247" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/CassandraAusten-HenryIV.jpg/330px-CassandraAusten-HenryIV.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/CassandraAusten-HenryIV.jpg/440px-CassandraAusten-HenryIV.jpg 2x" data-file-width="450" data-file-height="505" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:CassandraAusten-HenryIV.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait of <a href="/wiki/Henry_IV_of_England" title="Henry IV of England">Henry IV</a>. Declaredly written by "a partial, prejudiced, &amp; ignorant Historian", <i><a href="/wiki/The_History_of_England_(Jane_Austen)" class="mw-redirect" title="The History of England (Jane Austen)">The History of England</a></i> was illustrated by Austen\'s sister, Cassandra (c.&#160;1790).</div></div></div>\n<p>Among these works are a satirical novel in letters titled <i><a href="/wiki/Love_and_Freindship" title="Love and Freindship">Love and Freindship</a></i> &#32;&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Sic" title="Sic">sic</a></i>&#93;, written at age fourteen in 1790,<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> in which she mocked popular <a href="/wiki/Sentimental_novel" title="Sentimental novel">novels of sensibility</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>. The next year she wrote <i>The History of England</i>, a manuscript of thirty-four pages accompanied by thirteen watercolour miniatures by her sister, Cassandra. Austen\'s <i>History</i> parodied popular historical writing, particularly <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Goldsmith" title="Oliver Goldsmith">Oliver Goldsmith</a>\'s <i>History of England</i> (1764).<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> Honan speculates that not long after writing <i>Love and Freindship</i>&#32;&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Sic" title="Sic">sic</a></i>&#93;, Austen decided to "write for profit, to make stories her central effort", that is, to become a professional writer. When she was around eighteen years old Austen began to write longer, more sophisticated works.<sup id="cite_ref-Honan,_93_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Honan,_93-61">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In August 1792, aged seventeen, Austen started writing <i>Catharine or the Bower</i>, which presaged her mature work, especially <i>Northanger Abbey</i>; it was left unfinished and the story picked up in <i><a href="/wiki/Lady_Susan" title="Lady Susan">Lady Susan</a></i>, which Todd describes as less prefiguring than <i>Catharine</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> A year later, she began but abandoned a short play, later titled <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sir_Charles_Grandison_(Austen)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Sir Charles Grandison (Austen) (page does not exist)">Sir Charles Grandison or the happy Man, a comedy in 6 acts</a></i>, which she returned to and completed around 1800. This was a short parody of various school textbook abridgements of Austen\'s favourite contemporary novel, <i><a href="/wiki/The_History_of_Sir_Charles_Grandison" title="The History of Sir Charles Grandison">The History of Sir Charles Grandison</a></i> (1753), by <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Richardson" title="Samuel Richardson">Samuel Richardson</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="infobox" style="width:22em;float: left; clear: left; margin:0 1.0em 1.0em 0;"><caption>External video</caption><tbody><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;text-align: left">\n<img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/16px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png" width="16" height="16" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/24px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/32px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="128" data-file-height="128" /> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.c-span.org/video/?95776-1/jane-austen-life">Presentation by Claire Tomalin on <i>Jane Austen: A Life\'</i>, November 23, 1997</a>, <a href="/wiki/C-SPAN" title="C-SPAN">C-SPAN</a></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>When Austen became an aunt for the first time at age eighteen, she sent new-born niece Fanny-Catherine Austen-Knight "five short pieces of \xe2\x80\xa6 the Juvenilia now known collectively as \'Scraps\' .., purporting to be her \'Opinions and Admonitions on the conduct of Young Women\' ". For niece Jane-Anna-Elizabeth Austen (also born in 1793) Jane Austen wrote "two more \xe2\x80\x98Miscellanious [sic] Morsels\xe2\x80\x99, dedicating them to [Anna] on 2 June 1793, \'convinced that if you seriously attend to them, You will derive from them very important Instructions, with regard to your Conduct in Life.\' ".<sup id="cite_ref-Le_Faye_1993_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Le_Faye_1993-64">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> There is manuscript evidence that Austen continued to work on these pieces as late as 1811 (when she was 36), and that her niece and nephew, Anna and James Edward Austen, made further additions as late as 1814.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Between 1793 and 1795 (aged eighteen to twenty) Austen wrote <i><a href="/wiki/Lady_Susan" title="Lady Susan">Lady Susan</a></i>, a short <a href="/wiki/Epistolary_novel" title="Epistolary novel">epistolary novel</a>, usually described as her most ambitious and sophisticated early work.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> It is unlike any of Austen\'s other works. Austen biographer <a href="/wiki/Claire_Tomalin" title="Claire Tomalin">Claire Tomalin</a> describes the novella\'s heroine as a sexual predator who uses her intelligence and charm to manipulate, betray and abuse her lovers, friends and family. Tomalin writes:\n</p>\n<blockquote style="border-left: 3px solid #ccc;">\n<p>Told in letters, it is as neatly plotted as a play, and as cynical in tone as any of the most outrageous of the <a href="/wiki/Restoration_comedy" title="Restoration comedy">Restoration dramatists</a> who may have provided some of her inspiration&#160;... It stands alone in Austen\'s work as a study of an adult woman whose intelligence and force of character are greater than those of anyone she encounters.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n</blockquote>\n<p>According to Janet Todd, the model for the title character may have been Eliza de Feuillide, who inspired Austen with stories of her glamorous life and various adventures.  Eliza\'s French husband was guillotined in 1794; she married Jane\'s brother Henry Austen in 1797.<sup id="cite_ref-Todd4_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Todd4-32">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tom_Lefroy">Tom Lefroy</span></h3>\n<p>When Austen was twenty, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Langlois_Lefroy" title="Thomas Langlois Lefroy">Tom Lefroy</a>, a neighbour, visited Steventon from December 1795 to January 1796. He had just finished a university degree and was moving to London for training as a <a href="/wiki/Barrister" title="Barrister">barrister</a>. Lefroy and Austen would have been introduced at a ball or other neighbourhood social gathering, and it is clear from Austen\'s letters to Cassandra that they spent considerable time together: "I am almost afraid to tell you how my Irish friend and I behaved. Imagine to yourself everything most profligate and shocking in the way of dancing and sitting down together."<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Thomas_Langlois_Lefroy.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Thomas_Langlois_Lefroy.jpg/220px-Thomas_Langlois_Lefroy.jpg" width="220" height="222" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Thomas_Langlois_Lefroy.jpg/330px-Thomas_Langlois_Lefroy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Thomas_Langlois_Lefroy.jpg/440px-Thomas_Langlois_Lefroy.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1713" data-file-height="1731" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Thomas_Langlois_Lefroy.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Thomas_Langlois_Lefroy" title="Thomas Langlois Lefroy">Thomas Langlois Lefroy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lord_Chief_Justice_of_Ireland" title="Lord Chief Justice of Ireland">Lord Chief Justice of Ireland</a>, by <a href="/wiki/William_Henry_Mote" title="William Henry Mote">W. H. Mote</a> (1855); in old age, Lefroy admitted that he had been in love with Austen: "It was boyish love."<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Austen wrote in her first surviving letter to her sister Cassandra that Lefroy was a "very gentlemanlike, good-looking, pleasant young man".<sup id="cite_ref-halperin721_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-halperin721-70">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Five days later in another letter, Austen wrote that she expected an "offer" from her "friend" and that "I shall refuse him, however, unless he promises to give away his white coat", going on to write "I will confide myself in the future to Mr Tom Lefroy, for whom I don\'t give a sixpence" and refuse all others.<sup id="cite_ref-halperin721_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-halperin721-70">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> The next day, Austen wrote: "The day will come on which I flirt my last with Tom Lefroy and when you receive this it will be all over. My tears flow as I write at this melancholy idea".<sup id="cite_ref-halperin721_70-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-halperin721-70">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Halperin cautioned that Austen often satirised popular sentimental romantic fiction in her letters, and some of the statements about Lefroy may have been ironic. However, it is clear that Austen was genuinely attracted to Lefroy and subsequently none of her other suitors ever quite measured up to him.<sup id="cite_ref-halperin721_70-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-halperin721-70">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> The Lefroy family intervened and sent him away at the end of January. Marriage was impractical as both Lefroy and Austen must have known. Neither had any money, and he was dependent on a great-uncle in Ireland to finance his education and establish his legal career. If Tom Lefroy later visited Hampshire, he was carefully kept away from the Austens, and Jane Austen never saw him again.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> In November 1798, Lefroy was still on Austen\'s mind as she wrote to her sister she had tea with one of his relatives, wanted desperately to ask about him, but could not bring herself to raise the subject.<sup id="cite_ref-halperin722_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-halperin722-72">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h3><span id="Early_manuscripts_.281796.E2.80.931798.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_manuscripts_(1796\xe2\x80\x931798)">Early manuscripts (1796\xe2\x80\x931798)</span></h3>\n<p>After finishing <i>Lady Susan</i>, Austen began her first full-length novel <i>Elinor and Marianne</i>. Her sister remembered that it was read to the family "before 1796" and was told through a series of letters. Without surviving original manuscripts, there is no way to know how much of the original draft survived in the novel published anonymously in 1811 as <i><a href="/wiki/Sense_and_Sensibility" title="Sense and Sensibility">Sense and Sensibility</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Austen began a second novel, <i>First Impressions</i> (later published as <i><a href="/wiki/Pride_and_Prejudice" title="Pride and Prejudice">Pride and Prejudice</a></i>), in 1796. She completed the initial draft in August 1797, aged 21; as with all of her novels, Austen read the work aloud to her family as she was working on it and it became an "established favourite".<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> At this time, her father made the first attempt to publish one of her novels. In November 1797, George Austen wrote to <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Cadell_(publisher)" title="Thomas Cadell (publisher)">Thomas Cadell</a>, an established publisher in London, to ask if he would consider publishing <i>First Impressions</i>. Cadell returned Mr. Austen\'s letter, marking it "Declined by Return of Post". Austen may not have known of her father\'s efforts.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> Following the completion of <i>First Impressions</i>, Austen returned to <i>Elinor and Marianne</i> and from November 1797 until mid-1798, revised it heavily; she eliminated the epistolary format in favour of <a href="/wiki/Third-person_narrative" class="mw-redirect" title="Third-person narrative">third-person narration</a> and produced something close to <i>Sense and Sensibility</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> In 1797, Austen met her cousin (and future sister-in-law), Eliza de Feuillide, a French aristocrat whose first husband the Comte de Feuillide had been guillotined, causing her to flee to Britain, where she married Henry Austen.<sup id="cite_ref-King_page_2_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-King_page_2-77">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> The description of the execution of the Comte de Feuillide related by his widow left Austen with an intense horror of the French Revolution that lasted for the rest of her life.<sup id="cite_ref-King_page_2_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-King_page_2-77">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the middle of 1798, after finishing revisions of <i>Elinor and Marianne</i>, Austen began writing a third novel with the working title <i>Susan</i> \xe2\x80\x93 later <i><a href="/wiki/Northanger_Abbey" title="Northanger Abbey">Northanger Abbey</a></i> \xe2\x80\x93 a satire on the popular <a href="/wiki/Gothic_fiction" title="Gothic fiction">Gothic novel</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Austen completed her work about a year later. In early 1803, Henry Austen offered <i>Susan</i> to Benjamin Crosby, a London publisher, who paid \xc2\xa310 for the copyright. Crosby promised early publication and went so far as to advertise the book publicly as being "in the press", but did nothing more.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> The manuscript remained in Crosby\'s hands, unpublished, until Austen repurchased the copyright from him in 1816.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bath_and_Southampton">Bath and Southampton</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Royal_Crescent_1829.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/48/Royal_Crescent_1829.jpg/330px-Royal_Crescent_1829.jpg" width="330" height="230" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/48/Royal_Crescent_1829.jpg/495px-Royal_Crescent_1829.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/48/Royal_Crescent_1829.jpg/660px-Royal_Crescent_1829.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1187" data-file-height="826" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Royal_Crescent_1829.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Royal_Crescent" title="Royal Crescent">Royal Crescent</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bath,_Somerset" title="Bath, Somerset">Bath</a>, c. 1829</div></div></div>\n<p>In December 1800 George Austen unexpectedly announced his decision to retire from the ministry, leave Steventon, and move the family to 4, Sydney Place in <a href="/wiki/Bath,_Somerset" title="Bath, Somerset">Bath</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> While retirement and travel were good for the elder Austens, Jane Austen was shocked to be told she was moving from the only home she had ever known.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> An indication of her state of mind is her lack of productivity as a writer during the time she lived at Bath. She was able to make some revisions to <i>Susan</i>, and she began and then abandoned a new novel, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Watsons" title="The Watsons">The Watsons</a></i>, but there was nothing like the productivity of the years 1795\xe2\x80\x931799.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> Tomalin suggests this reflects a deep depression disabling her as a writer, but Honan disagrees, arguing Austen wrote or revised her manuscripts throughout her creative life, except for a few months after her father died.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;g&#93;</a></sup> It is often claimed that Austen was unhappy in Bath, which caused her to lose interest in writing, but it is just as possible that Austen\'s social life in Bath prevented her from spending much time writing novels.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> The critic Robert Irvine argued that if Austen spent more time writing novels when she was in the countryside, it might just been because she had more spare time as opposed to being more happy in the countryside as is often argued.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Furthermore, Austen frequently both moved and traveled over southern England during this period, which was hardly a conducive environment for writing a long novel.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> Austen sold the rights to publish <i>Susan</i> to a publisher Crosby &amp; Company, who paid her \xc2\xa310.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> The Crosby &amp; Company advertised <i>Susan</i>, but never published it.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> \n</p><p>The years from 1801 to 1804 are something of a blank space for Austen scholars as Cassandra destroyed all of her letters from her sister in this period for unknown reasons.<sup id="cite_ref-halperin729_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-halperin729-91">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> In December 1802 Austen received her only known proposal of marriage. She and her sister visited Alethea and Catherine Bigg, old friends who lived near <a href="/wiki/Basingstoke" title="Basingstoke">Basingstoke</a>. Their younger brother, Harris Bigg-Wither, had recently finished his education at Oxford and was also at home. Bigg-Wither proposed and Austen accepted. As described by Caroline Austen, Jane\'s niece, and Reginald Bigg-Wither, a descendant, Harris was not attractive \xe2\x80\x93 he was a large, plain-looking man who spoke little, stuttered when he did speak, was aggressive in conversation, and almost completely tactless. However, Austen had known him since both were young and the marriage offered many practical advantages to Austen and her family. He was the heir to extensive family estates located in the area where the sisters had grown up. With these resources, Austen could provide her parents a comfortable old age, give Cassandra a permanent home and, perhaps, assist her brothers in their careers. By the next morning, Austen realised she had made a mistake and withdrew her acceptance.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> No contemporary letters or diaries describe how Austen felt about this proposal.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Irvine described Bigg-Wither as a somebody who "...seems to have been a man very hard to like, let alone love".<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> \n</p><p>In 1814, Austen wrote a letter to her niece, Fanny Knight, who had asked for advice about a serious relationship, telling her that "having written so much on one side of the question, I shall now turn around &amp; entreat you not to commit yourself farther, &amp; not to think of accepting him unless you really do like him. Anything is to be preferred or endured rather than marrying without Affection".<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> The English scholar Douglas Bush wrote that Austen had "had a very high ideal of the love that should unite a husband and wife&#160;... All of her heroines&#160;... know in proportion to their maturity, the meaning of ardent love".<sup id="cite_ref-halperin732_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-halperin732-96">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> A possible autobiographical element in <i>Sense and Sensibility</i> occurs when Elinor Dashwood contemplates that "the worse and most irremediable of all evils, a connection for life" with an unsuitable man.<sup id="cite_ref-halperin732_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-halperin732-96">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:JaneAustenCassandraWatercolour.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/JaneAustenCassandraWatercolour.jpg/220px-JaneAustenCassandraWatercolour.jpg" width="220" height="287" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b8/JaneAustenCassandraWatercolour.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="288" data-file-height="376" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:JaneAustenCassandraWatercolour.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Watercolour of Jane Austen by her sister, <a href="/wiki/Cassandra_Austen" title="Cassandra Austen">Cassandra</a>, 1804.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>In 1804, while living in Bath, Austen started but did not complete her novel, <i>The Watsons</i>. The story centres on an invalid and impoverished clergyman and his four unmarried daughters. Sutherland describes the novel as "a study in the harsh economic realities of dependent women\'s lives".<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> Honan suggests, and Tomalin agrees, that Austen chose to stop work on the novel after her father died on 21 January 1805 and her personal circumstances resembled those of her characters too closely for her comfort.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Her father\'s relatively sudden death left Jane, Cassandra, and their mother in a precarious financial situation. Edward, James, Henry, and Francis Austen pledged to make annual contributions to support their mother and sisters.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> For the next four years, the family\'s living arrangements reflected their financial insecurity. They spent part of the time in rented quarters in Bath before leaving the city in June 1805 for a family visit to Steventon and <a href="/wiki/Godmersham_Park" title="Godmersham Park">Godmersham</a>. They moved for the autumn months to the newly fashionable seaside resort of <a href="/wiki/Worthing" title="Worthing">Worthing</a>, on the Sussex coast, where they resided at Stanford Cottage.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;h&#93;</a></sup> It was here that Austen is thought to have written her fair copy of <i>Lady Susan</i> and added its "Conclusion". In 1806 the family moved to <a href="/wiki/Southampton" title="Southampton">Southampton</a>, where they shared a house with Frank Austen and his new wife. A large part of this time they spent visiting various branches of the family.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 5 April 1809, about three months before the family\'s move to Chawton, Austen wrote an angry letter to Richard Crosby, offering him a new manuscript of <i>Susan</i> if needed to secure the immediate publication of the novel, and requesting the return of the original so she could find another publisher. Crosby replied that he had not agreed to publish the book by any particular time, or at all, and that Austen could repurchase the manuscript for the \xc2\xa310 he had paid her and find another publisher. She did not have the resources to buy the copyright back at that time,<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> but was able to purchase it in 1816.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chawton">Chawton</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jane_Austen_(House_in_Chawton)_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Jane_Austen_%28House_in_Chawton%29_2.jpg/300px-Jane_Austen_%28House_in_Chawton%29_2.jpg" width="300" height="187" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Jane_Austen_%28House_in_Chawton%29_2.jpg/450px-Jane_Austen_%28House_in_Chawton%29_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Jane_Austen_%28House_in_Chawton%29_2.jpg/600px-Jane_Austen_%28House_in_Chawton%29_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1905" data-file-height="1189" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jane_Austen_(House_in_Chawton)_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The cottage in <a href="/wiki/Chawton" title="Chawton">Chawton</a> where Austen lived during the last eight years of her life, now <a href="/wiki/Jane_Austen%27s_House_Museum" title="Jane Austen&#39;s House Museum">Jane Austen\'s House Museum</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Around early 1809 Austen\'s brother Edward offered his mother and sisters a more settled life \xe2\x80\x93 the use of a large cottage in <a href="/wiki/Chawton" title="Chawton">Chawton</a> village<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;i&#93;</a></sup> that was part of Edward\'s nearby estate, <a href="/wiki/Chawton_House" title="Chawton House">Chawton House</a>. Jane, Cassandra and their mother moved into Chawton cottage on 7 July 1809.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> Life was quieter in Chawton than it had been since the family\'s move to Bath in 1800. The Austens did not socialise with gentry and entertained only when family visited. Her niece Anna described the family\'s life in Chawton as "a very quiet life, according to our ideas, but they were great readers, and besides the housekeeping our aunts occupied themselves in working with the poor and in teaching some girl or boy to read or write."<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Published_author">Published author</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Styles_and_themes_of_Jane_Austen" title="Styles and themes of Jane Austen">Styles and themes of Jane Austen</a></div>\n<p>At the time, married British women did not have the legal power to sign contracts, and it was common for a woman wishing to publish to have a male relative represent her to sign the contract.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> Like most women authors at the time, Austen\'s books were published anonymously.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> At the time, the ideal roles for a woman were as wife and mother, and writing for women was regarded at best as a secondary form of activity; a woman who wished to be a full-time writer was felt to be degrading her femininity, so books by women were usually published anonymously in order to maintain the conceit that the female writer was only publishing as a sort of part-time job, and was not seeking to become a "literacy lioness" (i.e a celebrity).<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>  \n</p><p>During her time at Chawton, Jane Austen published four generally well-received novels. Through her brother Henry, the publisher <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Egerton_(publisher)" title="Thomas Egerton (publisher)">Thomas Egerton</a> agreed to publish <i><a href="/wiki/Sense_and_Sensibility" title="Sense and Sensibility">Sense and Sensibility</a></i>, which, like all of Jane Austen\'s novels except <i>Pride and Prejudice</i>, was published "on commission", that is, at the author\'s financial risk. When publishing on commission, publishers would advance the costs of publication, repay themselves as books were sold and then charge a 10% commission for each book sold, paying the rest to the author. If a novel did not recover its costs through sales, the author was responsible for them.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> The alternative to selling via commission was the selling the copyright, where an author received a one-time payment from the publisher for the manuscript, which occurred with <i>Pride and Prejudice</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> Austen\'s experience with <i>Susan</i> (the manuscript that became <i>Northanger Abbey</i>) where she sold the copyright to the publisher Crosby &amp; Sons for \xc2\xa310, who did not publish the book, forcing her to buy back the copyright in order to get her work published, left Austen leery of this method of publishing.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>  The final alternative, of selling by subscription, where a group of people would agree to buy a book in advance, was not an option for Austen as only authors who were well known or had an influential aristocratic patron who would recommend an up-coming book to their friends, could sell by subscription.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>  <i>Sense and Sensibility</i> appeared in October 1811, and was described as being written "By a Lady".<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> As it was sold on commission, Egerton used expensive paper and set the price at 15 shillings.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> \n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:192px;"><a href="/wiki/File:SenseAndSensibilityTitlePage.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/SenseAndSensibilityTitlePage.jpg/190px-SenseAndSensibilityTitlePage.jpg" width="190" height="311" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/SenseAndSensibilityTitlePage.jpg/285px-SenseAndSensibilityTitlePage.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/SenseAndSensibilityTitlePage.jpg/380px-SenseAndSensibilityTitlePage.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1472" data-file-height="2412" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:SenseAndSensibilityTitlePage.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>First edition title page from <i><a href="/wiki/Sense_and_Sensibility" title="Sense and Sensibility">Sense and Sensibility</a></i>, Austen\'s first published novel (1811)</div></div></div>\n<p>Reviews were favourable and the novel became fashionable among young aristocratic opinion-makers;<sup id="cite_ref-Honan_1987,_289\xe2\x80\x93290_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Honan_1987,_289\xe2\x80\x93290-118">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> the edition sold out by mid-1813. Austen\'s novels were published in larger editions than was normal for this period. The small size of the novel-reading public and the large costs associated with hand production (particularly the cost of handmade paper) meant that most novels were published in editions of 500 copies or less to reduce the risks to the publisher and the novelist. Even some of the most successful titles during this period were issued in editions of not more than 750 or 800 copies and later reprinted if demand continued. Austen\'s novels were published in larger editions, ranging from about 750 copies of <i>Sense and Sensibility</i> to about 2,000 copies of <i>Emma</i>. It is not clear whether the decision to print more copies than usual of Austen\'s novels was driven by the publishers or the author. Since all but one of Austen\'s books were originally published "on commission", the risks of overproduction were largely hers (or Cassandra\'s after her death) and publishers may have been more willing to produce larger editions than was normal practice when their own funds were at risk. Editions of popular works of non-fiction were often much larger.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> \n</p><p>Austen made \xc2\xa3140 from <i>Sense and Sensibility</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>  which provided her with some financial and psychological independence.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> After the success of <i>Sense and Sensibility</i>, all of Austen\'s subsequent books were billed as written "By the author of <i>Sense and Sensibility</i>" and Austen\'s name never appeared on her books during her lifetime.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> Egerton then published <i><a href="/wiki/Pride_and_Prejudice" title="Pride and Prejudice">Pride and Prejudice</a></i>, a revision of <i>First Impressions</i>, in January 1813. Austen sold the copyright to <i>Pride and Prejudice</i> to Egerton for \xc2\xa3110.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> To maximise profits, he used cheap paper and set the price at 18 shillings.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> He advertised the book widely and it was an immediate success, garnering three favourable reviews and selling well. Had Austen sold <i>Pride and Prejudice</i> on commission, she would had made a profit of \xc2\xa3475, or twice her father\'s annual income.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> By October 1813 Egerton was able to begin selling a second edition.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Mansfield_Park" title="Mansfield Park">Mansfield Park</a></i> was published by Egerton in May 1814. While <i>Mansfield Park</i> was ignored by reviewers, it was very popular with readers. All copies were sold within six months, and Austen\'s earnings on this novel were larger than for any of her other novels.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> \n</p><p>Unknown to Austen, her novels were translated into French and published in cheaply produced, pirated editions in France.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> The literary critic Noel King commented that given the prevailing rage in France at the time was for lush romantic fantasies, it is remarkable that her novels with the emphasis on everyday English life had any sort of a market in France.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> However, King cautioned that Austen\'s chief translator in France, Madame <a href="/wiki/Isabelle_de_Montolieu" title="Isabelle de Montolieu">Isabelle de Montolieu</a>, had only the most rudimentary knowledge of English, and her translations were more of "imitations" than translations proper, as Montolieu depended upon assistants to provide a summary, which she then translated into an embellished French that often radically altered Austen\'s plots and characters.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> The first of the Austen novels to be published that credited her as the author was in France, when <i>Persuasion</i> was published in 1821 as <i>La Famille Elliot ou L\'Ancienne Inclination</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Austen learned that the <a href="/wiki/George_IV_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="George IV of the United Kingdom">Prince Regent</a> admired her novels and kept a set at each of his residences.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;j&#93;</a></sup> In November 1815, the Prince Regent\'s librarian <a href="/wiki/James_Stanier_Clarke" title="James Stanier Clarke">James Stanier Clarke</a> invited Austen to visit the Prince\'s London residence and hinted Austen should dedicate the forthcoming <i><a href="/wiki/Emma_(novel)" title="Emma (novel)">Emma</a></i> to the Prince. Though Austen disliked the Prince Regent, she could scarcely refuse the request.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> Austen disapproved of the Prince Regent on the account of his womanising, gambling, drinking, spendthrift ways and generally disreputable behaviour.<sup id="cite_ref-halperin734_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-halperin734-135">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>  She later wrote <i><a href="/wiki/Plan_of_a_Novel,_according_to_hints_from_various_quarters" class="mw-redirect" title="Plan of a Novel, according to hints from various quarters">Plan of a Novel, according to hints from various quarters</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;(<a href="https://fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plan_of_a_Novel" class="extiw" title="fr:Plan of a Novel">fr</a>)</span></i>, a satiric outline of the "perfect novel" based on the librarian\'s many suggestions for a future Austen novel.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> Austen was greatly annoyed by Clarke\'s often pompous literary advice, and the <i>Plan of A Novel</i> parodying  Clarke was intended as her revenge for all of the unwanted letters she had received from the royal librarian.<sup id="cite_ref-halperin734_135-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-halperin734-135">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In mid-1815 Austen moved her work from Egerton to <a href="/wiki/John_Murray_(publisher)" title="John Murray (publisher)">John Murray</a>, a better known London publisher,<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;k&#93;</a></sup> who published <i>Emma</i> in December 1815 and a second edition of <i>Mansfield Park</i> in February 1816. <i>Emma</i> sold well but the new edition of <i>Mansfield Park</i> did poorly, and this failure offset most of the income from <i>Emma</i>. These were the last of Austen\'s novels to be published during her lifetime.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>While Murray prepared <i>Emma</i> for publication, Austen began <i>The Elliots</i>, later published as <i><a href="/wiki/Persuasion_(novel)" title="Persuasion (novel)">Persuasion</a></i>. She completed her first draft in July 1816. In addition, shortly after the publication of <i>Emma</i>, Henry Austen repurchased the copyright for <i>Susan</i> from Crosby. Austen was forced to postpone publishing either of these completed novels by family financial troubles. Henry Austen\'s bank failed in March 1816, depriving him of all of his assets, leaving him deeply in debt and losing Edward, James, and Frank Austen large sums. Henry and Frank could no longer afford the contributions they had made to support their mother and sisters.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Illness_and_death">Illness and death</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:192px;"><a href="/wiki/File:House_that_Jane_Austen_died_in_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/House_that_Jane_Austen_died_in_1.jpg/190px-House_that_Jane_Austen_died_in_1.jpg" width="190" height="285" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/House_that_Jane_Austen_died_in_1.jpg/285px-House_that_Jane_Austen_died_in_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/House_that_Jane_Austen_died_in_1.jpg/380px-House_that_Jane_Austen_died_in_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3456" data-file-height="5184" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:House_that_Jane_Austen_died_in_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>House in Winchester in which Austen lived her last days and died</div></div></div>\n<p>Austen was feeling unwell by early 1816, but ignored the warning signs. By the middle of that year, her decline was unmistakable, and she began a slow, irregular deterioration.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> The majority of biographers rely on Dr. Vincent Cope\'s 1964 <a href="/wiki/Retrospective_diagnosis" title="Retrospective diagnosis">retrospective diagnosis</a> and list her cause of death as <a href="/wiki/Addison%27s_disease" title="Addison&#39;s disease">Addison\'s disease</a>, although her final illness has also been described as resulting from <a href="/wiki/Hodgkin%27s_lymphoma" title="Hodgkin&#39;s lymphoma">Hodgkin\'s lymphoma</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;l&#93;</a></sup> When her uncle died and left his entire fortune to his wife, effectively disinheriting his relatives, she suffered a relapse, writing, "I am ashamed to say that the shock of my Uncle\'s Will brought on a relapse&#160;... but a weak Body must excuse weak Nerves".<sup id="cite_ref-Todd13_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Todd13-145">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>She continued to work in spite of her illness. Dissatisfied with the ending of <i>The Elliots</i>, she rewrote the final two chapters, which she finished on 6 August 1816.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;m&#93;</a></sup> In January 1817 Austen began <i>The Brothers</i> (titled <i><a href="/wiki/Sanditon" title="Sanditon">Sanditon</a></i> when published in 1925), and completed twelve chapters before stopping work in mid-March 1817, probably due to illness.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> Todd describes <i>Sanditon\'</i>s heroine, Diana Parker, as an "energetic invalid". In the novel, Austen mocked hypochondriacs and though she describes the heroine as "bilious", five days after abandoning the novel she wrote of herself that she was turning "every wrong colour" and living "chiefly on the sofa".<sup id="cite_ref-Todd13_145-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Todd13-145">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> She put down her pen on 18 March 1817, making a note of it.<sup id="cite_ref-Todd13_145-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Todd13-145">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Austen made light of her condition, describing it as "bile" and <a href="/wiki/Rheumatism" title="Rheumatism">rheumatism</a>. As her illness progressed, she experienced difficulty walking and lacked energy; by mid-April she was confined to bed. In May Cassandra and Henry brought her to <a href="/wiki/Winchester" title="Winchester">Winchester</a> for treatment, by which time she suffered agonising pain and welcomed death.<sup id="cite_ref-Todd13_145-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Todd13-145">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> Austen died in Winchester on 18 July 1817, at the age of 41. Henry, through his clerical connections, arranged for his sister to be buried in the north aisle of the nave of <a href="/wiki/Winchester_Cathedral" title="Winchester Cathedral">Winchester Cathedral</a>. The epitaph composed by her brother James praises Austen\'s personal qualities, expresses hope for her salvation and mentions the "extraordinary endowments of her mind", but does not explicitly mention her achievements as a writer.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Posthumous_publication">Posthumous publication</span></h2>\n<p>After Austen\'s death, Cassandra, Henry Austen and Murray arranged for the publication of <i>Persuasion</i> and <i>Northanger Abbey</i> as a set.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;n&#93;</a></sup> Henry Austen contributed a <i>Biographical Note</i> which for the first time identified his sister as the author of the novels. Tomalin describes it as "a loving and polished eulogy".<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> Sales were good for a year \xe2\x80\x93 only 321 copies remained unsold at the end of 1818.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1832 <a href="/wiki/Richard_Bentley_(publisher)" title="Richard Bentley (publisher)">Richard Bentley</a> purchased the remaining copyrights to all of her novels, and over the following winter published five illustrated volumes as part of his <i>Standard Novels</i> series. In October 1833, Bentley released the first collected edition of her works. Since then, Austen\'s novels have been continuously in print.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Genre_and_style">Genre and style</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Styles_and_themes_of_Jane_Austen" title="Styles and themes of Jane Austen">Styles and themes of Jane Austen</a></div>\n<p>Austen\'s works critique the <a href="/wiki/Sentimental_novel" title="Sentimental novel">sentimental novels</a> of the second half of the 18th century and are part of the transition to 19th-century literary realism.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;o&#93;</a></sup>  The earliest English novelists, Richardson, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Fielding" title="Henry Fielding">Henry Fielding</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tobias_Smollett" title="Tobias Smollett">Tobias Smollett</a>, were followed by the school of sentimentalists and <a href="/wiki/Romanticism#Romantic_literature" title="Romanticism">romantics</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Walter_Scott" title="Walter Scott">Walter Scott</a>, <a href="/wiki/Horace_Walpole" title="Horace Walpole">Horace Walpole</a>, <a href="/wiki/Clara_Reeve" title="Clara Reeve">Clara Reeve</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ann_Radcliffe" title="Ann Radcliffe">Ann Radcliffe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Laurence_Sterne" title="Laurence Sterne">Laurence Sterne</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Goldsmith" title="Oliver Goldsmith">Oliver Goldsmith</a>, whose style and genre Austen rejected, returning the novel on a "slender thread" to the tradition of Richardson and Fielding for a "realistic study of manners".<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> In the mid-20th century, literary critics <a href="/wiki/F._R._Leavis" title="F. R. Leavis">F. R. Leavis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ian_Watt" title="Ian Watt">Ian Watt</a> placed her in the tradition of Richardson and Fielding; both believe that she used their tradition of  "irony, realism and satire to form an author superior to both".<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Walter Scott noted Austen\'s "resistance to the trashy sensationalism of much of modern fiction \xe2\x80\x93 \'the ephemeral productions which supply the regular demand of watering places and circulating libraries\'".<sup id="cite_ref-Keymer21_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Keymer21-159">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> Yet her rejection of these genres is complex, as evidenced by <i>Northanger Abbey</i> and <i>Emma</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Keymer21_159-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Keymer21-159">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> Similar to <a href="/wiki/William_Wordsworth" title="William Wordsworth">William Wordsworth</a>, who excoriated the modern frantic novel in the "Preface" to his <i><a href="/wiki/Lyrical_Ballads" title="Lyrical Ballads">Lyrical Ballads</a></i> (1800), Austen distances herself from escapist novels; the discipline and innovation she demonstrates is similar to his, and she shows "that rhetorically less is artistically more."<sup id="cite_ref-Keymer21_159-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Keymer21-159">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> She eschewed popular Gothic fiction, stories of terror in which a heroine typically was stranded in a remote location, a castle or abbey (32 novels between 1784 and 1818 contain the word "abbey" in their title). Yet in <i>Northanger Abbey</i> she alludes to the trope, with the heroine, Catherine, anticipating a move to a remote locale.
