Rather than full-scale rejection or parody, Austen transforms the genre, juxtaposing reality, with descriptions of elegant rooms and modern comforts, against the heroine\'s "novel-fueled" desires.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> Nor does she completely denigrate Gothic fiction: instead she transforms settings and situations, such that the heroine is still imprisoned, yet her imprisonment is mundane and real \xe2\x80\x93 regulated manners and the strict rules of the ballroom.<sup id="cite_ref-Keymer29_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Keymer29-161">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> In <i>Sense and Sensibility</i> Austen presents characters who are more complex than in staple sentimental fiction, according to critic Keymer, who notes that although it is a parody of popular sentimental fiction, "Marianne in her sentimental histrionics responds to the calculating world&#160;... with a quite justifiable scream of female distress."<sup id="cite_ref-Keymer32_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Keymer32-162">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:250px;&#32;; font-size: 90%; background-color: #FFFFF0; padding:1.25em"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303512">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{margin:0.5em auto 0.8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft p,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright p{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:larger;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" \xe2\x80\x9c ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" \xe2\x80\x9d ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:360px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{min-width:100%;margin:0 0 0.8em!important;float:none!important}}</style>\n<div class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">The hair was curled, and the maid sent away, and Emma sat down to think and be miserable. It was a wretched business, indeed! Such an overthrow of everything she had been wishing for! Such a development of every thing most unwelcome!</div>\n<p><cite class="right-aligned" style="">\xe2\x80\x93 example of <a href="/wiki/Free_indirect_speech" title="Free indirect speech">free indirect speech</a>, Jane Austen,  <i><a href="/wiki/Emma_(novel)" title="Emma (novel)">Emma</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup></cite>\n</p>\n</div>\n<p>Richardson\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Pamela;_or,_Virtue_Rewarded" title="Pamela; or, Virtue Rewarded">Pamela</a></i>, the prototype for the sentimental novel, is a didactic love story with a happy ending, written at a time women were beginning to have the right to choose husbands and yet were restricted by social conventions.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> Austen attempted Richardson\'s epistolary style, but found the flexibility of narrative more conducive to her realism, a realism in which each conversation and gesture carries a weight of significance. The narrative style utilises <a href="/wiki/Free_indirect_speech" title="Free indirect speech">free indirect speech</a> \xe2\x80\x93 she was the first English novelist to do so extensively \xe2\x80\x93 through which she had the ability to present a character\'s thoughts directly to the reader and yet still retain narrative control. The style allows an author to vary discourse between the narrator\'s voice and values and those of the characters.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Austen had a natural ear for speech and dialogue, according to scholar <a href="/wiki/Mary_Lascelles_(literary_scholar)" class="mw-redirect" title="Mary Lascelles (literary scholar)">Mary Lascelles</a> "Few novelists can be more scrupulous than Jane Austen as to the phrasing and thoughts of their characters."<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup>  Techniques such as fragmentary speech suggest a character\'s traits and their tone;  "syntax and phrasing rather than vocabulary" is utilised to indicate social variants.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> Dialogue reveals a character\'s mood \xe2\x80\x93 frustration, anger, happiness \xe2\x80\x93 each treated differently and often through varying patterns of sentence structures. When Elizabeth Bennett rejects Darcy, her stilted speech and the convoluted sentence structure reveals that he has wounded her:<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<blockquote style="border-left: 3px solid #ccc;">\n<p>From the very beginning, from the first moment I may almost say, of my acquaintance with you, your manners impressing me with the fullest belief of your arrogance, your conceit, and your selfish disdain of the feelings of others, were such as to form that the groundwork of disapprobation, on which succeeding events have built so immovable a dislike. And I had not known you a month before I felt that you were the last man in the world whom I could ever be prevailed on to marry.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n</blockquote>\n<p>Austen\'s plots highlight women\'s traditional dependence on marriage to secure social standing and economic security.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> As an art form, the 18th-century novel lacked the seriousness of its equivalents from the 19th century, when novels were treated as "the natural vehicle for discussion and ventilation of what mattered in life".<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup>  Rather than delving too deeply into the psyche of her characters, Austen enjoys them and imbues them with humour, according to critic John Bayley. He believes that the well-spring of her wit and irony is her own attitude that comedy "is the saving grace of life".<sup id="cite_ref-Bayley26ff_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bayley26ff-172">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> Part of Austen\'s fame rests on the historical and literary significance that she was the first woman to write great comic novels. <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Johnson" title="Samuel Johnson">Samuel Johnson</a>\'s influence is evident, in that she follows his advice to write "a representation of life as may excite mirth".<sup id="cite_ref-Polhemus_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Polhemus-173">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Her humour comes from her modesty and lack of superiority, allowing her most successful characters, such as Elizabeth Bennet, to transcend the trivialities of life, which the more foolish characters are overly absorbed in.<sup id="cite_ref-Bayley26ff_172-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bayley26ff-172">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> Austen used comedy to explore the individualism of women\'s lives and gender relations, and she appears to have used it to find the goodness in life, often fusing it with "ethical sensibility", creating artistic tension. Critic Robert Polhemus writes,  "To appreciate the drama and achievement of Austen, we need to realize how deep was her passion for both reverence and ridicule&#160;... and her comic imagination reveals both the harmonies and the telling contradictions of her mind and vision as she tries to reconcile her satirical bias with her sense of the good."<sup id="cite_ref-Polhemus_173-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Polhemus-173">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reception">Reception</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Reception_history_of_Jane_Austen" title="Reception history of Jane Austen">Reception history of Jane Austen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Janeite" title="Janeite">Janeite</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jane_Austen_in_popular_culture" title="Jane Austen in popular culture">Jane Austen in popular culture</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Contemporaneous_responses">Contemporaneous responses</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:New-Monthly-Magazine-1816-25-p66-novels-inc-Austen-Emma-detail.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/New-Monthly-Magazine-1816-25-p66-novels-inc-Austen-Emma-detail.jpg/250px-New-Monthly-Magazine-1816-25-p66-novels-inc-Austen-Emma-detail.jpg" width="250" height="105" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/New-Monthly-Magazine-1816-25-p66-novels-inc-Austen-Emma-detail.jpg/375px-New-Monthly-Magazine-1816-25-p66-novels-inc-Austen-Emma-detail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/59/New-Monthly-Magazine-1816-25-p66-novels-inc-Austen-Emma-detail.jpg 2x" data-file-width="387" data-file-height="163" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:New-Monthly-Magazine-1816-25-p66-novels-inc-Austen-Emma-detail.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In 1816 the editors of <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_Monthly_Magazine" title="The New Monthly Magazine">The New Monthly Magazine</a></i> noted <i><a href="/wiki/Emma_(novel)" title="Emma (novel)">Emma</a>\'s</i> publication but chose not to review it.<sup id="ref_K_1" class="reference"><a href="#cnote_K">[K]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>As Austen\'s works were published anonymously, they brought her little personal renown. They were fashionable among opinion-makers, but were rarely reviewed.<sup id="cite_ref-Honan_1987,_289\xe2\x80\x93290_118-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Honan_1987,_289\xe2\x80\x93290-118">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> Most of the reviews were short and on balance favourable, although superficial and cautious.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SouthamVol1p1_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SouthamVol1p1-175">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> They most often focused on the moral lessons of the novels.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> Sir <a href="/wiki/Walter_Scott" title="Walter Scott">Walter Scott</a>, a leading novelist of the day, contributed one anonymously. Using the review as a platform to defend the then-disreputable genre of the novel, he praised Austen\'s realism.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> The other important early review was attributed to <a href="/wiki/Richard_Whately" title="Richard Whately">Richard Whately</a> in 1821. However, Whately denied having authored the review, which drew favourable comparisons between Austen and such acknowledged greats as <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homer</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">Shakespeare</a>, and praised the dramatic qualities of her narrative. Scott and Whately set the tone for almost all subsequent 19th-century Austen criticism.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="19th_century">19th century</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pickering_-_Greatbatch_-_Jane_Austen_-_Pride_and_Prejudice_-_She_then_told_him_what_Mr._Darcy_had_voluntarily_done_for_Lydia.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Pickering_-_Greatbatch_-_Jane_Austen_-_Pride_and_Prejudice_-_She_then_told_him_what_Mr._Darcy_had_voluntarily_done_for_Lydia.jpg/180px-Pickering_-_Greatbatch_-_Jane_Austen_-_Pride_and_Prejudice_-_She_then_told_him_what_Mr._Darcy_had_voluntarily_done_for_Lydia.jpg" width="180" height="301" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Pickering_-_Greatbatch_-_Jane_Austen_-_Pride_and_Prejudice_-_She_then_told_him_what_Mr._Darcy_had_voluntarily_done_for_Lydia.jpg/270px-Pickering_-_Greatbatch_-_Jane_Austen_-_Pride_and_Prejudice_-_She_then_told_him_what_Mr._Darcy_had_voluntarily_done_for_Lydia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Pickering_-_Greatbatch_-_Jane_Austen_-_Pride_and_Prejudice_-_She_then_told_him_what_Mr._Darcy_had_voluntarily_done_for_Lydia.jpg/360px-Pickering_-_Greatbatch_-_Jane_Austen_-_Pride_and_Prejudice_-_She_then_told_him_what_Mr._Darcy_had_voluntarily_done_for_Lydia.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3612" data-file-height="6044" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pickering_-_Greatbatch_-_Jane_Austen_-_Pride_and_Prejudice_-_She_then_told_him_what_Mr._Darcy_had_voluntarily_done_for_Lydia.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>One of the first two published illustrations of <i><a href="/wiki/Pride_and_Prejudice" title="Pride and Prejudice">Pride and Prejudice</a></i>, from the <a href="/wiki/Richard_Bentley_(publisher)" title="Richard Bentley (publisher)">Richard Bentley</a> edition.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> Caption reads: "She then told him [Mr Bennett] what Mr Darcy had voluntarily done for Lydia. He heard her with astonishment."</div></div></div>\n<p>Because Austen\'s novels did not conform to <a href="/wiki/Romanticism" title="Romanticism">Romantic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Victorian_literature" title="Victorian literature">Victorian</a> expectations that "powerful emotion [be] authenticated by an egregious display of sound and colour in the writing",<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup>  19th-century critics and audiences preferred the works of <a href="/wiki/Charles_Dickens" title="Charles Dickens">Charles Dickens</a> and <a href="/wiki/George_Eliot" title="George Eliot">George Eliot</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> In a rare sympathetic review, in this case of <i>Emma</i> in 1815, Sir <a href="/wiki/Walter_Scott" title="Walter Scott">Walter Scott</a> wrote that book displayed "the art of copying from nature as she really exists in the common walks of life, and presenting to the reader, instead of the splendid scenes from an imaginary world, a correct and striking representation of that which is daily taking place around him".<sup id="cite_ref-Litz,_A_pages_669_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Litz,_A_pages_669-182">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup>  Though Scott was positive, Austen\'s work did not match the prevailing aesthetic values of the Romantic zeitgeist.<sup id="cite_ref-Litz,_A_pages_669_182-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Litz,_A_pages_669-182">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> Her novels were republished in Britain from the 1830s and sold at a steady rate, but they were not bestsellers.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> The first French critic who paid notice to Austen was <a href="/wiki/Philar%C3%A8te_Chasles" title="Philar\xc3\xa8te Chasles">Philar\xc3\xa8te Chasles</a> who completely dismissed her as a writer, giving her two sentences in an 1842 essay on the influence of Sir Walter Scott, calling her a boring, imitative writer who wrote nothing of substance.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-184">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> Apart from Chasles, Austen was almost completely ignored in France until 1878.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_184-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-184">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Austen had many admiring readers in the 19th century, who considered themselves part of a literary elite. Philosopher and literary critic <a href="/wiki/George_Henry_Lewes" title="George Henry Lewes">George Henry Lewes</a> expressed this viewpoint in a series of enthusiastic articles published in the 1840s and 1850s.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> This theme continued later in the century with novelist <a href="/wiki/Henry_James" title="Henry James">Henry James</a>, who referred to Austen several times with approval and on one occasion ranked her with Shakespeare, <a href="/wiki/Miguel_de_Cervantes" title="Miguel de Cervantes">Cervantes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Henry_Fielding" title="Henry Fielding">Henry Fielding</a> as among "the fine painters of life".<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The publication of James Edward Austen-Leigh\'s <i><a href="/wiki/A_Memoir_of_Jane_Austen" title="A Memoir of Jane Austen">A Memoir of Jane Austen</a></i> in 1869 introduced Austen to a wider public as "dear aunt Jane", the respectable maiden aunt. Publication of the <i>Memoir</i> spurred the reissue of Austen\'s novels \xe2\x80\x93 the first popular editions were released in 1883 and fancy illustrated editions and collectors\' sets quickly followed.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> Author and critic <a href="/wiki/Leslie_Stephen" title="Leslie Stephen">Leslie Stephen</a> described the popular mania that started to develop for Austen in the 1880s as "Austenolatry". In 1878, the French critic L\xc3\xa9on Boucher published the essay <i>Le Roman Classique en Angleterre</i>, where he called Austen a "genius", which was the first time that epithet had been used in France to describe Austen.<sup id="cite_ref-auto1_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-188">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> The first proper translation of Austen into French that was completely faithful to the original occurred in 1899 when <a href="/wiki/F%C3%A9lix_F%C3%A9n%C3%A9on" title="F\xc3\xa9lix F\xc3\xa9n\xc3\xa9on">F\xc3\xa9lix F\xc3\xa9n\xc3\xa9on</a> translated <i>Northanger Abbey</i> into French as <i>Catherine Moreland</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-auto1_188-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-188">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> Around the start of the 20th century, members of the literary elite reacted against the popularisation of Austen. They referred to themselves as <i><a href="/wiki/Janeite" title="Janeite">Janeites</a></i> in order to distinguish themselves from the masses who did not properly understand her works. For example, Henry James responded negatively to what he described as "a beguiled infatuation" with Austen, a rising tide of public interest that exceeded Austen\'s "intrinsic merit and interest".<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> The American literary critic <a href="/wiki/A._Walton_Litz" title="A. Walton Litz">A. Walton Litz</a> noted that the "anti-Janites" in the 19th and 20th centuries comprise a formidable literary squad of Mark Twain, Henry James, Charlotte Bronte, D.H. Lawrence and Kingsley Amis, but in "every case the adverse judgement merely reveals the special limitations or eccentricities of the critic, leaving Jane Austen relatively untouched".<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern">Modern</span></h3>\n<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-style ambox-Tone" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/60px-Edit-clear.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/80px-Edit-clear.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="48" data-file-height="48" /></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section\'s <b>tone or style may not reflect the <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Writing_better_articles#Tone" title="Wikipedia:Writing better articles">encyclopedic tone</a> used on Wikipedia</b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> See Wikipedia\'s <a class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:333px;max-width:333px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:172px;max-width:172px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Winchester_Cathedral_view_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Winchester_Cathedral_view_1.jpg/170px-Winchester_Cathedral_view_1.jpg" width="170" height="256" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Winchester_Cathedral_view_1.jpg/255px-Winchester_Cathedral_view_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Winchester_Cathedral_view_1.jpg/340px-Winchester_Cathedral_view_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="969" data-file-height="1460" /></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:157px;max-width:157px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Wincath-11S7-9687.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Wincath-11S7-9687.jpg/155px-Wincath-11S7-9687.jpg" width="155" height="257" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Wincath-11S7-9687.jpg/233px-Wincath-11S7-9687.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Wincath-11S7-9687.jpg/310px-Wincath-11S7-9687.jpg 2x" data-file-width="604" data-file-height="1000" /></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent"><a href="/wiki/Winchester_Cathedral" title="Winchester Cathedral">Winchester Cathedral</a>, where Austen is buried, and her memorial gravestone in the nave of the Cathedral</div></div></div>\n<p>Several of Austen\'s works have been subject to academic study. The first dissertation on Austen was published in 1883, by George Pellew, a student at Harvard University.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup> The first examination came from a 1911 essay by Oxford Shakespearean scholar <a href="/wiki/A._C._Bradley" title="A. C. Bradley">A. C. Bradley</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> In his essay, Bradley groups Austen\'s novels into "early" and "late" works, a distinction still used by scholars today.<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> The first academic books devoted to Austen in France was <i>Jane Austen</i> by Paul and Kate Rague published in 1914, where the Ragues set out to explain why French critics and readers should take Austen seriously.<sup id="cite_ref-auto1_188-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-188">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> The same year, L\xc3\xa9onie Villard published <i>Jane Austen, Sa Vie et Ses Oeuvres</i>, which was originally her PhD thesis, marking the first time that Austen had subjected to a serious academic study in France.<sup id="cite_ref-auto1_188-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-188">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> The second examination in English was R. W. Chapman\'s 1923 edition of Austen\'s collected works. Not only was it the first scholarly edition of Austen\'s works, it was also the first scholarly edition of any English novelist. The Chapman text has remained the basis for all subsequent published editions of Austen\'s works.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>With the publication in 1939 of <a href="/wiki/Mary_Lascelles_(literary_scholar)" class="mw-redirect" title="Mary Lascelles (literary scholar)">Mary Lascelles\'s</a> <i>Jane Austen and Her Art</i>, the academic study of Austen took hold.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> Lascelles\'s innovative work included an analysis of the books Austen read and the effect of her reading on her work, an extended analysis of Austen\'s style, and her "narrative art". Concern arose that academics were taking over Austen criticism and that it was becoming increasingly esoteric, a debate that has continued since.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The period since World War II has seen more scholarship on Austen using a diversity of critical approaches, including <a href="/wiki/Feminist_theory" title="Feminist theory">feminist theory</a>, and perhaps most controversially, <a href="/wiki/Postcolonialism" title="Postcolonialism">postcolonial theory</a>. The continuing disconnection between the popular appreciation of Austen, particularly by modern <a href="/wiki/Janeite" title="Janeite">Janeites</a>, and the academic appreciation of Austen has widened considerably.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> After the founding of the People\'s Republic of China in 1949, Austen was in disfavor with the authorities who only wanted Western authors to be published in translation whose work could be presented as representing the West in a negative light, and Austen was regarded as too "frivolous" for this purpose.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> As hostile as the treatment of Austen was in the 1950s, it paled besides the treatment of her books during the "Great Proletarian Cultural Revolution" in China between 1966\xe2\x80\x9369, when Austen was banned as a "British bourgeois imperialist" author.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> In the late 1970s, Austen was allowed to be published in China, where her popularity with readers confounded the authorities who had trouble understanding that people sometimes want to read books for enjoyment instead of dialectical purposes.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> A sign of the way that Austen can still spark debate can be seen when the American English professor Gene Koppel mentioned in a lecture that Austen and her family were "Tories of the deepest dye" [the Tories were the conservative party while the Whigs were the liberal party], a statement which greatly upset many of Koppel\'s liberal students, who much to his amusement, complained to him how was it possible that Austen was a conservative.<sup id="cite_ref-Koppel_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Koppel-201">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> The conservative Koppel noted several feminist authors such as Claudia Johnson and  Mollie Sandock were claiming Austen for their own cause.<sup id="cite_ref-Koppel_201-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Koppel-201">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> Citing the work of <a href="/wiki/Hans-Georg_Gadamer" title="Hans-Georg Gadamer">Hans-Georg Gadamer</a>, Koppel argued that different people can and do react to the same work of literature in different ways as art is always a subjective discipline as various people have their standards for evaluating literature.<sup id="cite_ref-Koppel_201-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Koppel-201">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> As such, Koppel argued that competing interpretations of Austen\'s work, provided that they are grounded in readings of her work are all equally valid, and so it is equally possible to see Austen as a feminist critiquing Regency society and as a conservative upholding the values of Regency society.<sup id="cite_ref-Koppel_201-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Koppel-201">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Adaptations">Adaptations</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Jane_Austen_in_popular_culture" title="Jane Austen in popular culture">Jane Austen in popular culture</a></div>\n<p>Austen\'s novels have resulted in sequels, prequels and adaptations of almost every type, from <a href="/wiki/Softcore_pornography" title="Softcore pornography">soft-core pornography</a> to fantasy. From the 19th century, her family members published conclusions to her incomplete novels, and by 2000 there were over 100 printed adaptations.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> The first dramatic adaptation of Austen was published in 1895, Rosina Filippi\'s <i>Duologues and Scenes from the Novels of Jane Austen: Arranged and Adapted for Drawing-Room Performance</i>, and Filippi was also responsible for the first professional stage adaptation, <i>The Bennets</i> (1901).<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> The first film adaptation was the <a href="/wiki/Pride_and_Prejudice_(1940_film)" title="Pride and Prejudice (1940 film)">1940 MGM production of <i>Pride and Prejudice</i></a> starring <a href="/wiki/Laurence_Olivier" title="Laurence Olivier">Laurence Olivier</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greer_Garson" title="Greer Garson">Greer Garson</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> BBC television dramatisations since the 1970s have attempted to adhere meticulously to Austen\'s plots, characterisations and settings.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> The British critic Robert Irvine noted that in American film adaptations of Austen\'s novels, starting with the 1940 version of <i>Pride and Prejudice</i> and continuing on to today, class is subtly downplayed as the United States is officially an egalitarian nation where all people are equal and the society of Regency England depicted by Austen that is grounded in a hierarchy based upon the ownership of land and the antiquity of the family name is one that Americans cannot embrace in its entirety.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>From 1995 a large number of Austen adaptations began to appear, with <a href="/wiki/Ang_Lee" title="Ang Lee">Ang Lee</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Sense_and_Sensibility_(film)" title="Sense and Sensibility (film)">film of <i>Sense and Sensibility</i></a>, for which screenwriter and star <a href="/wiki/Emma_Thompson" title="Emma Thompson">Emma Thompson</a> won an <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award" class="mw-redirect" title="Academy Award">Academy Award</a>, and the BBC\'s immensely popular TV mini-series <i><a href="/wiki/Pride_and_Prejudice_(1995_TV_series)" title="Pride and Prejudice (1995 TV series)">Pride and Prejudice</a></i>, starring <a href="/wiki/Jennifer_Ehle" title="Jennifer Ehle">Jennifer Ehle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Colin_Firth" title="Colin Firth">Colin Firth</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> A 2005 British production of <a href="/wiki/Pride_%26_Prejudice_(2005_film)" title="Pride &amp; Prejudice (2005 film)"><i>Pride and Prejudice</i></a>, directed by <a href="/wiki/Joe_Wright" title="Joe Wright">Joe Wright</a> and starring <a href="/wiki/Keira_Knightley" title="Keira Knightley">Keira Knightley</a> and <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Macfadyen" title="Matthew Macfadyen">Matthew Macfadyen</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup> was followed in 2007 by <a href="/wiki/ITV_(TV_network)" title="ITV (TV network)">ITV</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Mansfield_Park_(2007_film)" title="Mansfield Park (2007 film)">Mansfield Park</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Northanger_Abbey_(2007_film)" title="Northanger Abbey (2007 film)">Northanger Abbey</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Persuasion_(2007_film)" title="Persuasion (2007 film)">Persuasion</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup> and in 2016 by <i><a href="/wiki/Love_%26_Friendship" title="Love &amp; Friendship">Love &amp; Friendship</a></i>, a film version of <i>Lady Susan</i> that borrowed the title of Austen\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Love_and_Freindship" title="Love and Freindship">Love and Freindship</a></i> [sic].<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="List_of_works">List of works</span></h2>\n<div class="div-col columns column-width" style="-moz-column-width: The original sketch, according to relatives who knew Jane Austen well, was not a good likeness.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-5">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Oliver MacDonagh says that <i>Sense and Sensibility</i> "may well be the first English realistic novel" based on its detailed and accurate portrayal of what he calls "getting and spending" in an English gentry family.<sup id="cite_ref-MacDonagh_1991,_65,_136\xe2\x80\x93137_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacDonagh_1991,_65,_136\xe2\x80\x93137-4">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-11">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> Important details about the Austen family were almost certainly elided by intention, such as mention of Austen\'s brother George, whose undiagnosed developmental challenges led the family to send him away from home; the two brothers sent away to the navy at an early age; or mention of the sisters\' wealthy Aunt Leigh-Perrot, arrested and tried on charges of larceny.<sup id="cite_ref-Nokes_1998_1_2_Fergus_2005_3_4_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nokes_1998_1_2_Fergus_2005_3_4-10">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-18"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-18">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Irene Collins estimates that when George Austen took up his duties as rector in 1764, Steventon comprised no more than about thirty families.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-34"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-34">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Philadelphia had returned from India in 1765 and taken up residence in London; when her husband returned to India to replenish their income, she stayed in England.  He died in India in 1775, with Philadelphia unaware until the news reached her a year later, fortuitously as George and Cassandra were visiting. See Le Faye, 29\xe2\x80\x9336</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-41"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-41">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For social conventions among the gentry generally, see Collins (1994), 105</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-85"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-85">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> Doody agrees with Tomalin; see Doody, "Jane Austen, that disconcerting child", in Alexander and McMaster 2005, 105.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-101"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-101">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Austen\'s observations of early Worthing probably helped inspire her final but unfinished novel, <i><a href="/wiki/Sanditon" title="Sanditon">Sanditon</a></i>, the story of an up-and-coming seaside resort in Sussex.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-106"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-106">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Chawton had a population of 417 at the census of 1811.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-133"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-133">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Prince Regent\'s admiration was by no means reciprocated. gentry family.<sup id="cite_ref-MacDonagh_1991,_65,_136\xe2\x80\x93137_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacDonagh_1991,_65,_136\xe2\x80\x93137-4">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li Man, a Comedy in 6 acts</i>. For more information see Southam (1986), 187\xe2\x80\x93189.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-212"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-212">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This list of the juvenilia is taken from <i>The Works of Jane Austen</i>.
